class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Great_Recession_sidebar" title="Template talk:Great Recession sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Great_Recession_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The Great Recession met the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">IMF</a> criteria for being a <a href="/wiki/Global_recession" title="Global recession">global recession</a> only in the single calendar year 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-New_IMF_Global_Recession_definition_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_IMF_Global_Recession_definition-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IMF_WEO_2009_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMF_WEO_2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> That IMF definition requires a <i>decline in annual real <a href="/wiki/Gross_world_product" title="Gross world product">world GDP</a> per&#x2011;capita <small></small></i>. Despite the fact that quarterly data are being used as <a href="/wiki/Recession" title="Recession">recession</a> definition criteria by all <a href="/wiki/G-20_major_economies" class="mw-redirect" title="G-20 major economies">G20 members</a>, representing 85% of the <a href="/wiki/Gross_world_product" title="Gross world product">world GDP</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> the International Monetary Fund (IMF) has decided\xe2\x80\x94in the absence of a complete data set\xe2\x80\x94not to declare/measure global recessions according to quarterly GDP data. The <i>seasonally adjusted <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">PPP</a>&#x2011;weighted real GDP</i> for the G20&#x2011;zone, however, is a good indicator for the world GDP, and it was measured to have suffered a direct <a href="/wiki/Quarter_on_quarter" title="Quarter on quarter">quarter on quarter</a> decline during the three quarters from Q3&#x2011;2008 until Q1&#x2011;2009, which more accurately mark when the recession took place at the global level.<sup id="cite_ref-stats.oecd.org_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stats.oecd.org-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According  to the U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Bureau_of_Economic_Research" title="National Bureau of Economic Research">National Bureau of Economic Research</a> (the official arbiter of U.S. recessions) the recession began in December 2007 and ended in June 2009, and thus extended over eighteen months.<sup id="cite_ref-nber.org_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nber.org-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The years leading up to the crisis were characterized by an exorbitant rise in asset prices and associated boom in economic demand.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Further, the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Shadow_banking_system" title="Shadow banking system">shadow banking system</a> (i.e., non-depository financial institutions such as investment banks) had grown to rival the depository system yet was not subject to the same regulatory oversight, making it vulnerable to a <a href="/wiki/Bank_run" title="Bank run">bank run</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>US mortgage-backed <a href="/wiki/Securities" class="mw-redirect" title="Securities">securities</a>, which had risks that were hard to assess, were marketed around the world, as they offered higher yields than U.S. government bonds. Many of these securities were backed by subprime mortgages, which collapsed in value when the U.S. housing bubble burst during 2006 and homeowners began to default on their mortgage payments in large numbers starting in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The emergence of <a href="/wiki/Subprime_lending" title="Subprime lending">sub-prime loan</a> losses in 2007 began the crisis and exposed other risky loans and over-inflated asset prices. With loan losses mounting and the fall of <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers</a> on September 15, 2008, a major panic broke out on the inter-bank loan market. There was the equivalent of a bank run on the <a href="/wiki/Shadow_banking_system" title="Shadow banking system">shadow banking system</a>,  resulting in many large and well established <a href="/wiki/Investment_bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Investment bank">investment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commercial_bank" title="Commercial bank">commercial</a> banks in the United States and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> suffering huge losses and even facing bankruptcy, resulting in massive public financial assistance (government bailouts).<sup id="cite_ref-KrugmanGeithner1_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KrugmanGeithner1-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The global recession that followed resulted in a sharp drop in <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a>, rising <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a> and slumping commodity prices.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Several economists predicted that recovery might not appear until 2011 and that the recession would be the worst since the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> of the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Economist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Krugman" title="Paul Krugman">Paul Krugman</a> once commented on this as seemingly the beginning of "a second Great Depression."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Governments and central banks responded with <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_policy" title="Fiscal policy">fiscal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monetary_policy" title="Monetary policy">monetary policies</a> to stimulate national economies and reduce financial system risks. The recession has renewed interest in <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%9309_Keynesian_resurgence" title="2008\xe2\x80\x9309 Keynesian resurgence">Keynesian economic ideas</a> on how to combat recessionary conditions.  Economists advise that the stimulus should be withdrawn as soon as the economies recover enough to "chart a path to sustainable growth".<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The distribution of household incomes in the United States has become more unequal during the post-2008 <a href="/wiki/Economic_recovery" title="Economic recovery">economic recovery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Appelbaum_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Appelbaum-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Income_inequality_in_the_United_States" title="Income inequality in the United States">Income inequality in the United States</a> has grown from 2005 to 2012 in more than 2 out of 3 metropolitan areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Chokshi_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chokshi-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Median household wealth fell 35% in the US, from $106,591 to $68,839 between 2005 and 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Kurtzleben_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurtzleben-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Causes_of_the_Great_Recession" title="Causes of the Great Recession">Causes of the Great Recession</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Great_Asset_Bubble.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/The_Great_Asset_Bubble.svg/250px-The_Great_Asset_Bubble.svg.png" width="250" height="283" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/The_Great_Asset_Bubble.svg/375px-The_Great_Asset_Bubble.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/The_Great_Asset_Bubble.svg/500px-The_Great_Asset_Bubble.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="566" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Great_Asset_Bubble.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The great asset bubble:<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <br /><b>1.</b> Central banks\' gold reserves: $0.845 trillion. <br /><b>2.</b> M0 (paper money): $3.9 trillion. <br /><b>3.</b> Traditional (fractional reserve) banking assets: $39 trillion. <br /><b>4.</b> Shadow banking assets: $62 trillion. <br /><b>5.</b> Other assets: $290 trillion. <br /><b>6.</b> Bail-out money (early 2009): $1.9 trillion.</div></div></div> <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008" title="Financial crisis of 2007\xe2\x80\x932008">Financial crisis of 2007\xe2\x80\x932008</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Panel_reports">Panel reports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Panel reports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The majority report provided by U.S. <a href="/wiki/Financial_Crisis_Inquiry_Commission" title="Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission">Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission</a>, composed of six Democratic and four Republican appointees, reported its findings in January 2011. It concluded that "the crisis was avoidable and was caused by:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Widespread failures in financial regulation, including the Federal Reserve\'s failure to stem the tide of toxic mortgages;</li>\n<li>Dramatic breakdowns in corporate governance including too many financial firms acting recklessly and taking on too much risk;</li>\n<li>An explosive mix of excessive borrowing and risk by households and Wall Street that put the financial system on a collision course with crisis;</li>\n<li>Key policy makers ill prepared for the crisis, lacking a full understanding of the financial system they oversaw; and systemic breaches in accountability and ethics at all levels."<sup id="cite_ref-stanford1_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanford1-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>There were two Republican dissenting FCIC reports. One of them, signed by three Republican appointees, concluded that there were multiple causes. In his separate dissent to the majority and minority opinions of the FCIC, Commissioner <a href="/wiki/Peter_J._Wallison" title="Peter J. Wallison">Peter J. Wallison</a> of the <a href="/wiki/American_Enterprise_Institute" title="American Enterprise Institute">American Enterprise Institute</a> (AEI) primarily blamed U.S. housing policy, including the actions of <a href="/wiki/Fannie_Mae" title="Fannie Mae">Fannie</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Mac" title="Freddie Mac">Freddie</a>, for the crisis. He wrote: "When the bubble began to deflate in mid-2007, the low quality and high risk loans engendered by government policies failed in unprecedented numbers."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In its "Declaration of the Summit on Financial Markets and the World Economy," dated November 15, 2008, leaders of the <a href="/wiki/G20_major_economies" class="mw-redirect" title="G20 major economies">Group of 20</a> cited the following causes:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>During a period of strong global growth, growing capital flows, and prolonged stability earlier this decade, market participants sought higher yields without an adequate appreciation of the risks and failed to exercise proper due diligence. At the same time, weak underwriting standards, unsound risk management practices, increasingly complex and opaque financial products, and consequent excessive leverage combined to create vulnerabilities in the system. Policy-makers, regulators and supervisors, in some advanced countries, did not adequately appreciate and address the risks building up in financial markets, keep pace with financial innovation, or take into account the systemic ramifications of domestic regulatory actions.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Narratives">Narratives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Narratives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._Fixed_Investment_as_Pct_GDP.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/U.S._Fixed_Investment_as_Pct_GDP.png/350px-U.S._Fixed_Investment_as_Pct_GDP.png" width="350" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/U.S._Fixed_Investment_as_Pct_GDP.png/525px-U.S._Fixed_Investment_as_Pct_GDP.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/U.S._Fixed_Investment_as_Pct_GDP.png/700px-U.S._Fixed_Investment_as_Pct_GDP.png 2x" data-file-width="2520" data-file-height="1512" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._Fixed_Investment_as_Pct_GDP.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. residential and non-residential investment fell relative to GDP during the crisis</div></div></div>\n<p>There are several "narratives" attempting to place the causes of the recession into context, with overlapping elements. Five such narratives include:\n</p>\n<ol><li>There was the equivalent of a <a href="/wiki/Bank_run" title="Bank run">bank run</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Shadow_banking_system" title="Shadow banking system">shadow banking system</a>, which includes investment banks and other non-depository financial entities. This system had grown to rival the depository system in scale yet was not subject to the same regulatory safeguards. Its failure disrupted the flow of credit to consumers and corporations.<sup id="cite_ref-KrugmanGeithner1_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KrugmanGeithner1-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-frbatlanta.org_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frbatlanta.org-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The U.S. economy was being driven by a housing bubble. When it burst, private residential investment (i.e., housing construction) fell by nearly four percent. GDP and consumption enabled by bubble-generated housing wealth also slowed. This created a gap in annual demand (GDP) of nearly $1 trillion. The U.S. government was unwilling to make up for this private sector shortfall.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Record levels of <a href="/wiki/Household_debt" title="Household debt">household debt</a> accumulated in the decades preceding the crisis resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Balance_sheet_recession" title="Balance sheet recession">balance sheet recession</a> (similar to <a href="/wiki/Debt_deflation" title="Debt deflation">debt deflation</a>) once housing prices began falling in 2006. Consumers began paying off debt, which reduces their consumption, slowing down the economy for an extended period while debt levels are reduced.<sup id="cite_ref-KrugmanGeithner1_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KrugmanGeithner1-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mian_and_Sufi2014_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mian_and_Sufi2014-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>U.S. government policies encouraged home ownership even for those who could not afford it, contributing to lax lending standards, unsustainable housing price increases, and indebtedness.<sup id="cite_ref-Sowell_2009_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sowell_2009-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Wealthy and middle-class <a href="/wiki/Flipping" title="Flipping">house flippers</a> with mid-to-good credit scores created a <a href="/wiki/Economic_bubble" title="Economic bubble">speculative bubble</a> in house prices, and then wrecked local housing markets and financial institutions after they defaulted on their debt en masse.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>\n<p>Underlying narratives #1-3 is a hypothesis that growing <a href="/wiki/Income_inequality_in_the_United_States" title="Income inequality in the United States">income inequality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Household_income_in_the_United_States" title="Household income in the United States">wage stagnation</a> encouraged families to increase their <a href="/wiki/Household_debt" title="Household debt">household debt</a> to maintain their desired living standard, fueling the bubble. Further, this greater share of income flowing to the top increased the political power of business interests, who used that power to <a href="/wiki/Deregulation" title="Deregulation">deregulate</a> or limit regulation of the shadow banking system.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Narrative #5 challenges the popular claim that subprime borrowers with shoddy credit caused the crisis by buying homes they couldn\'t afford.  This narrative is supported by new research showing that the biggest growth of mortgage debt during the U.S. housing boom came from those with good credit scores in the middle and top of the credit score distribution\xe2\x80\x94and that these borrowers accounted for a disproportionate share of defaults.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_imbalances_and_debt_bubbles">Trade imbalances and debt bubbles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Trade imbalances and debt bubbles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Private_Debt_to_GDP_by_Sector.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/US_Private_Debt_to_GDP_by_Sector.png/350px-US_Private_Debt_to_GDP_by_Sector.png" width="350" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/US_Private_Debt_to_GDP_by_Sector.png/525px-US_Private_Debt_to_GDP_by_Sector.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/US_Private_Debt_to_GDP_by_Sector.png/700px-US_Private_Debt_to_GDP_by_Sector.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Private_Debt_to_GDP_by_Sector.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. households and financial businesses significantly increased borrowing (leverage) in the years leading up to the crisis</div></div></div>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> wrote in July 2012 that the inflow of investment dollars required to fund the U.S. trade deficit was a major cause of the housing bubble and financial crisis: "The trade deficit, less than 1% of GDP in the early 1990s, hit 6% in 2006. That deficit was financed by inflows of foreign savings, in particular from East Asia and the Middle East. Much of that money went into dodgy mortgages to buy overvalued houses, and the financial crisis was the result."<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 2008, NPR explained in their <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Award" title="Peabody Award">Peabody Award</a> winning program "<a href="/wiki/The_Giant_Pool_of_Money" title="The Giant Pool of Money">The Giant Pool of Money</a>" that a vast inflow of savings from developing nations flowed into the mortgage market, driving the U.S. housing bubble. This pool of fixed income savings increased from around $35 trillion in 2000 to about $70 trillion by 2008. NPR explained this money came from various sources, "[b]ut the main headline is that all sorts of poor countries became kind of rich, making things like TVs and selling us oil. China, India, Abu Dhabi, Saudi Arabia made a lot of money and banked it."<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Describing the crisis in Europe, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Krugman" title="Paul Krugman">Paul Krugman</a> wrote in February 2012 that: "What we\'re basically looking at, then, is a balance of payments problem, in which capital flooded south after the creation of the euro, leading to overvaluation in southern Europe."<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monetary_policy">Monetary policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Monetary policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Another narrative about the origin has been focused on the respective parts played by the public monetary policy (in the US notably) and by the practices of private financial institutions.  In the U.S., mortgage funding was unusually decentralised, opaque, and competitive, and it is believed that competition between lenders for revenue and market share contributed to declining underwriting standards and risky lending.<sup id="cite_ref-ssrn.com_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssrn.com-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While Alan Greenspan\'s role as <a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Federal_Reserve" class="mw-redirect" title="Chairman of the Federal Reserve">Chairman of the Federal Reserve</a> has been widely discussed (the main point of controversy remains the lowering of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_funds_rate" title="Federal funds rate">Federal funds rate</a> to 1% for more than a year, which, according to <a href="/wiki/Austrian_School" title="Austrian School">Austrian theorists</a>, injected huge amounts of "easy" credit-based money into the financial system and created an unsustainable economic boom),<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> there is also the argument that Greenspan\'s actions in the years 2002\xe2\x80\x932004 were actually motivated by the need to take the U.S. economy out of the <a href="/wiki/Early_2000s_recession" title="Early 2000s recession">early 2000s recession</a> caused by the bursting of the <a href="/wiki/Dot-com_bubble" title="Dot-com bubble">dot-com bubble</a>\xe2\x80\x94although by doing so he did not help avert the crisis, but only postpone it.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_private_debt_levels">High private debt levels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: High private debt levels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png/250px-U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png/375px-U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png/500px-U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>US household debt relative to disposable income and GDP.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Change_in_household_debt_-_v1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Change_in_household_debt_-_v1.png/250px-Change_in_household_debt_-_v1.png" width="250" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Change_in_household_debt_-_v1.png/375px-Change_in_household_debt_-_v1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Change_in_household_debt_-_v1.png/500px-Change_in_household_debt_-_v1.png 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Change_in_household_debt_-_v1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. Changes in Household Debt as a percentage of GDP for 1989-2016.  Homeowners paying down debt for 2009-2012 was a headwind to the recovery. Economist <a href="/wiki/Carmen_Reinhart" title="Carmen Reinhart">Carmen Reinhart</a> explained that this behavior tends to slow recoveries from financial crises relative to typical recessions.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Another narrative focuses on high levels of private debt in the US economy.  USA <a href="/wiki/Household_debt" title="Household debt">household debt</a> as a percentage of annual <a href="/wiki/Disposable_personal_income" class="mw-redirect" title="Disposable personal income">disposable personal income</a> was 127% at the end of 2007, versus 77% in 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-The_End_of_the_Affair_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_End_of_the_Affair-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Faced with increasing mortgage payments as their adjustable rate mortgage payments increased, households began to default in record numbers, rendering mortgage-backed securities worthless. High private debt levels also impact growth by making recessions deeper and the following recovery weaker.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Reich" title="Robert Reich">Robert Reich</a> claims the amount of debt in the US economy can be traced to economic inequality, assuming that middle-class wages remained stagnant while wealth concentrated at the top, and households "pull equity from their homes and overload on debt to maintain living standards."<sup id="cite_ref-huff_inequality_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huff_inequality-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The IMF reported in April 2012: "Household debt soared in the years leading up to the downturn. In advanced economies, during the five years preceding 2007, the ratio of household debt to income rose by an average of 39 percentage points, to 138 percent. In Denmark, Iceland, Ireland, the Netherlands, and Norway, debt peaked at more than 200 percent of household income. A surge in household debt to historic highs also occurred in emerging economies such as Estonia, Hungary, Latvia, and Lithuania. The concurrent boom in both house prices and the stock market meant that household debt relative to assets held broadly stable, which masked households\' growing exposure to a sharp fall in asset prices. When house prices declined, ushering in the global financial crisis, many households saw their wealth shrink relative to their debt, and, with less income and more unemployment, found it harder to meet mortgage payments. By the end of 2011, real house prices had fallen from their peak by about 41% in Ireland, 29% in Iceland, 23% in Spain and the United States, and 21% in Denmark. Household defaults, underwater mortgages (where the loan balance exceeds the house value), foreclosures, and fire sales are now endemic to a number of economies. Household <a href="/wiki/Deleveraging" title="Deleveraging">deleveraging</a> by paying off debts or defaulting on them has begun in some countries. It has been most pronounced in the United States, where about two-thirds of the debt reduction reflects defaults."<sup id="cite_ref-imf.org_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-imf.org-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-recession_warnings">Pre-recession warnings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Pre-recession warnings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The onset of the economic crisis took most people by surprise. A 2009 paper identifies twelve economists and commentators who, between 2000 and 2006, predicted a recession based on the collapse of the then-booming housing market in the United States:<sup id="cite_ref-Bezemer_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bezemer-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dean_Baker" title="Dean Baker">Dean Baker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wynne_Godley" title="Wynne Godley">Wynne Godley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fred_Harrison_(author)" title="Fred Harrison (author)">Fred Harrison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Hudson_(economist)" title="Michael Hudson (economist)">Michael Hudson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Janszen" title="Eric Janszen">Eric Janszen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Med_Jones" title="Med Jones">Med Jones</a> <sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Steve_Keen" title="Steve Keen">Steve Keen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jakob_Broechner_Madsen" class="mw-redirect" title="Jakob Broechner Madsen">Jakob Br\xc3\xb8chner Madsen</a>, Jens Kjaer S\xc3\xb8rensen, <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Richeb%C3%A4cher" title="Kurt Richeb\xc3\xa4cher">Kurt Richeb\xc3\xa4cher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nouriel_Roubini" title="Nouriel Roubini">Nouriel Roubini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Schiff" title="Peter Schiff">Peter Schiff</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Shiller" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Shiller">Robert Shiller</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bezemer_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bezemer-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Housing_bubbles">Housing bubbles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Housing bubbles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FCIC_-_Housing_Bubbles_in_Multiple_Countries_2002-2008.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/FCIC_-_Housing_Bubbles_in_Multiple_Countries_2002-2008.png/250px-FCIC_-_Housing_Bubbles_in_Multiple_Countries_2002-2008.png" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/FCIC_-_Housing_Bubbles_in_Multiple_Countries_2002-2008.png/375px-FCIC_-_Housing_Bubbles_in_Multiple_Countries_2002-2008.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/FCIC_-_Housing_Bubbles_in_Multiple_Countries_2002-2008.png/500px-FCIC_-_Housing_Bubbles_in_Multiple_Countries_2002-2008.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FCIC_-_Housing_Bubbles_in_Multiple_Countries_2002-2008.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Housing price appreciation in selected countries, 2002\xe2\x80\x932008</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Real_estate_bubble" title="Real estate bubble">Real estate bubble</a></div>\n<p>By 2007, real estate bubbles were still under way in many parts of the world,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> especially in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_housing_bubble" title="United States housing bubble">United States</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ssrn.com_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssrn.com-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_property_bubble" class="mw-redirect" title="British property bubble">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_property_bubble" title="Spanish property bubble">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Netherlands" class="mw-redirect" title="The Netherlands">The Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_property_bubble" title="New Zealand property bubble">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_property_bubble" title="Irish property bubble">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_property_bubble" title="Polish property bubble">Poland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic states</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> U.S. Federal Reserve Chairman <a href="/wiki/Alan_Greenspan" title="Alan Greenspan">Alan Greenspan</a> said in mid-2005 that "at a minimum, there\'s a little \'froth\' [in the U.S. housing market]...it\'s hard not to see that there are a lot of local bubbles".<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">Economist</a></i> newspaper, writing at the same time, went further, saying "the worldwide rise in house prices is the biggest bubble in history".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Real estate bubbles are (by definition of the word "bubble") followed by a price decrease (also known as a <i>housing price crash</i>) that can result in many owners holding <a href="/wiki/Negative_equity" title="Negative equity">negative equity</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Mortgage_loan" title="Mortgage loan">mortgage</a> debt higher than the current value of the property).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Increases_in_uncertainty">Increases in uncertainty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Increases in uncertainty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Increases in uncertainty can depress investment, or consumption. The 2007\xe2\x80\x932009 recession represents the most striking episode of heightened uncertainty since 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ineffective_or_inappropriate_regulation">Ineffective or inappropriate regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ineffective or inappropriate regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulations_encouraging_lax_lending_standards">Regulations encouraging lax lending standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Regulations encouraging lax lending standards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Several analysts, such as Peter Wallison and Edward Pinto of the American Enterprise Institute, have asserted that private lenders were encouraged to relax lending standards by government affordable housing policies.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> They cite The Housing and Community Development Act of 1992, which initially required that 30 percent or more of Fannie\'s and Freddie\'s loan purchases be related to affordable housing. The legislation gave HUD the power to set future requirements, and eventually (under the Bush Administration) a 56 percent minimum was established.<sup id="cite_ref-Wallison_1_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wallison_1-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> To fulfil the requirements, Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac established programs to purchase $5 trillion in affordable housing loans,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> and encouraged lenders to relax underwriting standards to produce those loans.<sup id="cite_ref-Wallison_1_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wallison_1-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>These critics also cite, as inappropriate regulation, "The National Homeownership Strategy: Partners in the American Dream ("Strategy"), which was compiled in 1995 by Henry Cisneros, President Clinton\'s HUD Secretary. In 2001, the independent research company, Graham Fisher &amp; Company, stated: "While the underlying initiatives of the [Strategy] were broad in content, the main theme ... was the relaxation of credit standards."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Community Reinvestment Act (CRA) is also identified as one of the causes of the recession, by some critics. They contend that lenders relaxed lending standards in an effort to meet CRA commitments, and they note that publicly announced CRA loan commitments were massive, totaling $4.5 trillion in the years between 1994 and 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, the Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission (FCIC) concluded that Fannie &amp; Freddie "were not a primary cause" of the crisis and that CRA was not a factor in the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-stanford1_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanford1-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Further, since housing bubbles appeared in multiple countries in Europe as well, the FCIC Republican minority dissenting report also concluded that U.S. housing policies were not a robust explanation for a wider global housing bubble.<sup id="cite_ref-stanford1_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanford1-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>  The view that U.S. government housing policy was a primary cause of the crisis has been widely disputed,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Paul_Krugman" title="Paul Krugman">Paul Krugman</a> referring to it as "imaginary history."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Derivatives">Derivatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Derivatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Author <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lewis_(author)" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael Lewis (author)">Michael Lewis</a> wrote that a type of derivative called a <a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swap" title="Credit default swap">credit default swap</a> (CDS) enabled speculators to stack bets on the same mortgage securities. This is analogous to allowing many persons to buy insurance on the same house. Speculators that bought CDS protection were betting that significant mortgage security defaults would occur, while the sellers (such as <a href="/wiki/AIG" class="mw-redirect" title="AIG">AIG</a>) bet they would not. An unlimited amount could be wagered on the same housing-related securities, provided buyers and sellers of the CDS could be found.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> When massive defaults occurred on underlying mortgage securities, companies like AIG that were selling CDS were unable to perform their side of the obligation and defaulted; U.S. taxpayers paid over $100 billion to global financial institutions to honor AIG obligations, generating considerable outrage.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_Lanman_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_Lanman-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Derivatives such as CDS were unregulated or barely regulated. Several sources have noted the failure of the US government to supervise or even require transparency of the <a href="/wiki/Financial_instruments" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial instruments">financial instruments</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Derivative_(finance)" title="Derivative (finance)">derivatives</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-devils_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-devils-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-faiola_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faiola-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\nA 2008 investigative article in the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Post">Washington Post</a> found that leading government officials at the time (Federal Reserve Board Chairman  <a href="/wiki/Alan_Greenspan" title="Alan Greenspan">Alan Greenspan</a>, Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/Robert_Rubin" title="Robert Rubin">Robert Rubin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">SEC</a> Chairman <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Levitt" title="Arthur Levitt">Arthur Levitt</a>)  vehemently opposed any regulation of <a href="/wiki/Derivative_(finance)" title="Derivative (finance)">derivatives</a>. In 1998 <a href="/wiki/Brooksley_E._Born" class="mw-redirect" title="Brooksley E. Born">Brooksley E. Born</a>, head of the <a href="/wiki/Commodity_Futures_Trading_Commission" title="Commodity Futures Trading Commission">Commodity Futures Trading Commission</a>, put forth a policy paper asking for feedback from regulators, lobbyists, legislators on the question of whether derivatives should be reported, sold through a central facility, or whether capital requirements should be required of their buyers. Greenspan, Rubin, and Levitt pressured her to withdraw the paper and Greenspan persuaded <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> to pass a resolution preventing CFTC from regulating derivatives for another six months&#160;\xe2\x80\x94 when Born\'s term of office would expire.<sup id="cite_ref-devils_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-devils-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately, it was the collapse of a specific kind of derivative, the <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed security</a>, that triggered the economic crisis of 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-faiola_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faiola-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shadow_banking_system">Shadow banking system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Shadow banking system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Securitization_Market_Activity.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Securitization_Market_Activity.png/250px-Securitization_Market_Activity.png" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Securitization_Market_Activity.png/375px-Securitization_Market_Activity.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Securitization_Market_Activity.png/500px-Securitization_Market_Activity.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Securitization_Market_Activity.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Securitization markets were impaired during the crisis.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Paul_Krugman" title="Paul Krugman">Paul Krugman</a> wrote in 2009 that the <a href="/wiki/Bank_run" title="Bank run">run</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Shadow_banking_system" title="Shadow banking system">shadow banking system</a> was the "core of what happened" to cause the crisis. "As the shadow banking system expanded to rival or even surpass conventional banking in importance, politicians and government officials should have realised that they were re-creating the kind of financial vulnerability that made the Great Depression possible&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 and they should have responded by extending regulations and the financial safety net to cover these new institutions. Influential figures should have proclaimed a simple rule: anything that does what a bank does, anything that has to be rescued in crises the way banks are, should be regulated like a bank." He referred to this lack of controls as "malign neglect."<sup id="cite_ref-Krugman_2009_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krugman_2009-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During 2008, three of the largest U.S. investment banks either went bankrupt (<a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers</a>) or were sold at fire sale prices to other banks (<a href="/wiki/Bear_Stearns" title="Bear Stearns">Bear Stearns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch</a>). The investment banks were not subject to the more stringent regulations applied to depository banks. These failures exacerbated the instability in the global financial system. The remaining two investment banks, <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a>, potentially facing failure, opted to become commercial banks, thereby subjecting themselves to more stringent regulation but receiving access to credit via the Federal Reserve.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Further, <a href="/wiki/American_International_Group" title="American International Group">American International Group</a> (AIG) had insured mortgage-backed and other securities but was not required to maintain sufficient reserves to pay its obligations when debtors defaulted on these securities.  AIG was contractually required to post additional collateral with many creditors and counter-parties, touching off controversy when over $100&#160;billion of U.S. taxpayer money was paid out to major global financial institutions on behalf of AIG. While this money was legally owed to the banks by AIG (under agreements made via <a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swap" title="Credit default swap">credit default swaps</a> purchased from AIG by the institutions), a number of Congressmen and media members expressed outrage that taxpayer money was used to bail out banks.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_Lanman_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_Lanman-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nEconomist <a href="/wiki/Gary_Gorton" title="Gary Gorton">Gary Gorton</a> wrote in May 2009: </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Unlike the historical banking panics of the 19th and early 20th centuries, the current banking panic is a wholesale panic, not a retail panic. In the earlier episodes, depositors ran to their banks and demanded cash in exchange for their checking accounts. Unable to meet those demands, the banking system became insolvent. The current panic involved financial firms "running" on other financial firms by not renewing sale and repurchase agreements (repo) or increasing the repo margin ("haircut"), forcing massive deleveraging, and resulting in the banking system being insolvent.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote><p>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Financial_Crisis_Inquiry_Commission" title="Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission">Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission</a> reported in January 2011: </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In the early part of the 20th century, we erected a series of protections&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 the Federal Reserve as a <a href="/wiki/Lender_of_last_resort" title="Lender of last resort">lender of last resort</a>, federal deposit insurance, ample regulations&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 to provide a bulwark against the panics that had regularly plagued America\'s banking system in the 19th century. Yet, over the past 30-plus years, we permitted the growth of a shadow banking system&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 opaque and laden with short term debt&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 that rivaled the size of the traditional banking system. Key components of the market&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 for example, the multitrillion-dollar repo lending market, off-balance-sheet entities, and the use of over-the-counter derivatives&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 were hidden from view, without the protections we had constructed to prevent financial meltdowns. We had a 21st-century financial system with 19th-century safeguards.<sup id="cite_ref-stanford1_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanford1-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Systemic_crisis">Systemic crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Systemic crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The financial crisis and the recession have been described as a symptom of another, deeper crisis by a number of economists. For example, <a href="/wiki/Ravi_Batra" title="Ravi Batra">Ravi Batra</a> argues that growing inequality of <a href="/wiki/Financial_capitalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial capitalism">financial capitalism</a> produces speculative bubbles that burst and result in depression and major <a href="/wiki/The_Downfall_of_Capitalism_and_Communism" title="The Downfall of Capitalism and Communism">political changes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Feminist economists <a href="/wiki/Ailsa_McKay" title="Ailsa McKay">Ailsa McKay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Margunn_Bj%C3%B8rnholt" title="Margunn Bj\xc3\xb8rnholt">Margunn Bj\xc3\xb8rnholt</a> argue that the financial crisis and the response to it revealed a crisis of ideas in mainstream economics and within the economics profession, and call for a reshaping of both the economy, economic theory and the economics profession. They argue that such a reshaping should include new advances within <a href="/wiki/Feminist_economics" title="Feminist economics">feminist economics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecological_economics" title="Ecological economics">ecological economics</a> that take as their starting point the socially responsible, sensible and accountable subject in creating an economy and economic theories that fully acknowledge care for each other as well as the planet.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_the_Great_Recession" title="Effects of the Great Recession">Effects of the Great Recession</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_the_United_States">Effects on the United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Effects on the United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._economic_recovery_scorecard.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/U.S._economic_recovery_scorecard.png/300px-U.S._economic_recovery_scorecard.png" width="300" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/U.S._economic_recovery_scorecard.png/450px-U.S._economic_recovery_scorecard.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/U.S._economic_recovery_scorecard.png/600px-U.S._economic_recovery_scorecard.png 2x" data-file-width="1408" data-file-height="793" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._economic_recovery_scorecard.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Several major U.S. economic variables had recovered from the 2007-2009 <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">Subprime mortgage crisis</a> and Great Recession by the 2013-2014 time period.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._GDP_Contribution_to_Change_2007-2009.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/U.S._GDP_Contribution_to_Change_2007-2009.png/300px-U.S._GDP_Contribution_to_Change_2007-2009.png" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/U.S._GDP_Contribution_to_Change_2007-2009.png/450px-U.S._GDP_Contribution_to_Change_2007-2009.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/U.S._GDP_Contribution_to_Change_2007-2009.png/600px-U.S._GDP_Contribution_to_Change_2007-2009.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._GDP_Contribution_to_Change_2007-2009.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. Real GDP&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 Contributions to Percent Change by Component 2007-2009</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession_in_the_United_States" title="Great Recession in the United States">Great Recession in the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_in_the_United_States" title="Unemployment in the United States">Unemployment in the United States</a>, and <a href="/wiki/National_debt_of_the_United_States" title="National debt of the United States">National debt of the United States</a></div>\n<p>The Great Recession had a significant economic and political impact on the United States.  While the recession technically lasted from December 2007-June 2009 (the nominal GDP trough), many important economic variables did not regain pre-recession (November or Q4 2007) levels until 2011-2016. For example, real GDP fell $650 billion (4.3%) and did not recover its $15 trillion pre-recession level until Q3 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>  Household net worth, which reflects the value of both stock markets and housing prices, fell $11.5 trillion (17.3%) and did not regain its pre-recession level of $66.4 trillion until Q3 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The number of persons with jobs (total non-farm payrolls) fell 8.6 million (6.2%) and did not regain the pre-recession level of 138.3 million until May 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>  The unemployment rate peaked at 10.0% in October 2009 and did not return to its pre-recession level of 4.7% until May 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A key dynamic slowing the recovery was that both individuals and businesses paid down debts for several years, as opposed to borrowing and spending or investing as had historically been the case. This shift to a <a href="/wiki/Sectoral_balances" title="Sectoral balances">private sector surplus</a> drove a sizable government deficit.<sup id="cite_ref-NPI_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPI-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> However, the federal government held spending at about $3.5 trillion from fiscal years 2009-2014 (thereby decreasing it as a percent of GDP), a form of austerity. Then-Fed Chair <a href="/wiki/Ben_Bernanke" title="Ben Bernanke">Ben Bernanke</a> explained during November 2012 several of the economic headwinds that slowed the recovery:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The housing sector did not rebound, as was the case in prior recession recoveries, as the sector was severely damaged during the crisis. Millions of foreclosures had created a large surplus of properties and consumers were paying down their debts rather than purchasing homes.</li>\n<li>Credit for borrowing and spending by individuals (or investing by corporations) was not readily available as banks paid down their debts.</li>\n<li>Restrained government spending following initial stimulus efforts (i.e., austerity) was not sufficient to offset private sector weaknesses.<sup id="cite_ref-Bernanke_Recovery_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bernanke_Recovery-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>On the political front, widespread anger at <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">banking bailouts</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_Recovery_and_Reinvestment_Act_of_2009" title="American Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009">stimulus</a> measures (begun by President Bush 43 and continued or expanded by President Obama) with few consequences for banking leadership, were a factor in driving the country politically rightward starting in 2010. Examples include the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Tea_Party_movement" title="Tea Party movement">Tea Party</a> and the loss of Democratic majorities in subsequent elections. President Obama declared the bailout measures started under the Bush Administration and continued during his Administration as completed and mostly profitable as of December 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> As of January 2018, bailout funds had been fully recovered by the government, when interest on loans is taken into consideration. A total of $626B was invested, loaned, or granted due to various bailout measures, while $390B had been returned to the Treasury. The Treasury had earned another $323B in interest on bailout loans, resulting in an $87B profit.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Some have argued the Great Recession was also an important factor in the rise of populist sentiment that resulted in the election of President Trump in 2016, although his election also had important <a href="/wiki/Identity_politics" title="Identity politics">identity politics</a> elements.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-independent_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_Europe">Effects on Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Effects on Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/European_debt_crisis" title="European debt crisis">European debt crisis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austerity" title="Austerity">Austerity</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurozone_Countries_Public_Debt_to_GDP_Ratio_2010_vs._2011.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Eurozone_Countries_Public_Debt_to_GDP_Ratio_2010_vs._2011.png/220px-Eurozone_Countries_Public_Debt_to_GDP_Ratio_2010_vs._2011.png" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Eurozone_Countries_Public_Debt_to_GDP_Ratio_2010_vs._2011.png/330px-Eurozone_Countries_Public_Debt_to_GDP_Ratio_2010_vs._2011.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Eurozone_Countries_Public_Debt_to_GDP_Ratio_2010_vs._2011.png/440px-Eurozone_Countries_Public_Debt_to_GDP_Ratio_2010_vs._2011.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurozone_Countries_Public_Debt_to_GDP_Ratio_2010_vs._2011.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Public Debt to GDP Ratio for Selected European Countries - 2008 to 2011. Source Data: Eurostat</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurozone-structural1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Eurozone-structural1.jpg/220px-Eurozone-structural1.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Eurozone-structural1.jpg/330px-Eurozone-structural1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Eurozone-structural1.jpg/440px-Eurozone-structural1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="859" data-file-height="553" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurozone-structural1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relationship between fiscal tightening (austerity) in <a href="/wiki/Eurozone_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurozone countries">Eurozone countries</a> with their GDP growth rate, 2008\xe2\x80\x932012<sup id="cite_ref-martin_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-martin-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The crisis in Europe generally progressed from banking system crises to sovereign debt crises, as many countries elected to bail out their banking systems using taxpayer money.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Greece was different in that it faced large public debts rather than problems within its banking system. Several countries received bailout packages from the <a href="/wiki/European_troika" title="European troika">troika</a> (European Commission, European Central Bank, International Monetary Fund), which also implemented a series of emergency measures.\n</p><p>Many European countries embarked on austerity programs, reducing their budget deficits relative to GDP from 2010 to 2011. For example, according to the <i><a href="/wiki/CIA_World_Factbook" class="mw-redirect" title="CIA World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a></i> Greece improved its budget deficit from 10.4% GDP in 2010 to 9.6% in 2011. Iceland, Italy, Ireland, Portugal, France, and Spain also improved their budget deficits from 2010 to 2011 relative to GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-cia.gov_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Eurostat1_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eurostat1-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, with the exception of Germany, each of these countries had public-debt-to-GDP ratios that increased (i.e., worsened) from 2010 to 2011, as indicated in the chart at right. Greece\'s public-debt-to-GDP ratio increased from 143% in 2010 to 165% in 2011<sup id="cite_ref-cia.gov_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>  to 185% in 2014. This indicates that despite improving budget deficits, GDP growth was not sufficient to support a decline (improvement) in the debt-to-GDP ratio for these countries during this period. <a href="/wiki/Eurostat" title="Eurostat">Eurostat</a> reported that the debt to GDP ratio for the 17 Euro area countries together was 70.1% in 2008, 79.9% in 2009, 85.3% in 2010, and 87.2% in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Eurostat1_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eurostat1-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the <i>CIA World Factbook</i>, from 2010 to 2011, the unemployment rates in Spain, Greece, Italy, Ireland, Portugal, and the UK increased. France had no significant changes, while in Germany and Iceland the unemployment rate declined.<sup id="cite_ref-cia.gov_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Eurostat reported that Eurozone unemployment reached record levels in September 2012 at 11.6%, up from 10.3% the prior year. Unemployment varied significantly by country.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Economist <a href="/wiki/Martin_Wolf" title="Martin Wolf">Martin Wolf</a> analysed the relationship between cumulative GDP growth from 2008-2012 and total reduction in budget deficits due to austerity policies (see chart at right) in several European countries during April 2012. He concluded that: "In all, there is no evidence here that large fiscal contractions [budget deficit reductions] bring benefits to confidence and growth that offset the direct effects of the contractions. They bring exactly what one would expect: small contractions bring recessions and big contractions bring depressions." Changes in budget balances (deficits or surpluses) <a href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_determination" title="Coefficient of determination">explained</a> approximately 53% of the change in GDP, according to the equation derived from the IMF data used in his analysis.<sup id="cite_ref-martin_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-martin-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Economist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Krugman" title="Paul Krugman">Paul Krugman</a> analysed the relationship between GDP and reduction in budget deficits for several European countries in April 2012 and concluded that austerity was slowing growth, similar to Martin Wolf. He also wrote: "...&#160;this also implies that 1 euro of austerity yields only about 0.4 euros of reduced deficit, even in the short run. No wonder, then, that the whole austerity enterprise is spiraling into disaster."<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">decision to leave the European Union</a> in 2016 has been partly attributed to the after-effects of the Great Recession on the country.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Countries_that_avoided_recession">Countries that avoided recession</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Countries that avoided recession">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> are the only two members of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> to have avoided a GDP recession during the years affected by the Great Recession. As of December 2009, the Polish economy had not entered recession nor even contracted, while its IMF 2010 GDP growth forecast of 1.9 percent was expected to be upgraded.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Analysts have identified several causes for the positive economic development in Poland: Extremely low levels of bank lending and a relatively very small mortgage market; the relatively recent dismantling of EU trade barriers and the resulting surge in demand for Polish goods since 2004; Poland being the recipient of direct EU funding since 2004; lack of over-dependence on a single export sector; a tradition of government fiscal responsibility; a relatively large internal market; the free-floating Polish <a href="/wiki/Zloty" class="mw-redirect" title="Zloty">zloty</a>; low labour costs attracting continued foreign direct investment; economic difficulties at the start of the decade, which prompted austerity measures in advance of the world crisis.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>While <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> experienced slowing growth, they did not enter recessions.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> narrowly avoided technical recession in the first quarter of 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> stated in mid September that South Korea could be the only large <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> country to avoid recession for the whole of 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> It was the only developed economy to expand in the first half of 2009.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> avoided a technical recession after experiencing only one quarter of negative growth in the fourth quarter of 2008, with GDP returning to positive in the first quarter of 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The financial crisis did not affect developing countries to a great extent. Experts see several reasons: Africa was not affected because it is not fully integrated in the world market. Latin America and Asia seemed better prepared, since they have experienced crises before. In Latin America, for example, banking laws and regulations are very stringent. Bruno Wenn of the German DEG suggests that Western countries could learn from these countries when it comes to regulations of financial markets.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline_of_effects">Timeline of effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Recession&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Timeline of effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Great_Recession" title="Timeline of the Great Recession">Timeline of the Great Recession</a></div>\n<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Timeline_of_the_Great_Recession" title="Template:Timeline of the Great Recession"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Timeline_of_the_Great_Recession" title="Template talk:Timeline of the Great Recession"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Timeline_of_the_Great_Recession&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div>\n<p>The table below displays all national recessions appearing in 2006-2013 (for the 71 countries with available data), according to the common recession definition, saying that a recession occurred whenever seasonally adjusted <a href="/wiki/Real_GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="Real GDP">real GDP</a> contracts quarter on quarter, through minimum two consecutive quarters. Only 11 out of the 71 listed countries with quarterly GDP data (Poland, Slovakia, Moldova, India, China, South Korea, Indonesia, Australia, Uruguay, Colombia and Bolivia) escaped a recession in this time period.\n</p><p>The few recessions appearing early in 2006-07 are commonly never associated to be part of the Great Recession, which is illustrated by the fact that only two countries (Iceland and Jamaica) were in recession in Q4-2007. \n</p><p>One year before the maximum, in Q1-2008, only six countries were in recession (Iceland, Sweden, Finland, Ireland, Portugal and New Zealand). The number of countries in recession was 25 in Q2&#x2011;2008, 39 in Q3&#x2011;2008 and 53 in Q4&#x2011;2008. At the steepest part of the Great Recession in Q1&#x2011;2009, a total of 59 out of 71 countries were simultaneously in recession. The number of countries in recession was 37 in Q2&#x2011;2009, 13 in Q3&#x2011;2009 and 11 in Q4&#x2011;2009. One year after the maximum, in Q1&#x2011;2010, only seven countries were in recession (Greece, Croatia, Romania, Iceland, Jamaica, Venezuela and Belize).\n</p><p>The recession data for the overall <a href="/wiki/G-20_major_economies" class="mw-redirect" title="G-20 major economies">G20-zone</a> (representing 85% of all <a href="/wiki/Gross_world_product" title="Gross world product">GWP</a>), depict that the Great Recession existed as a <a href="/wiki/Global_recession" title="Global recession">global recession</a> throughout Q3&#x2011;2008 until Q1&#x2011;2009.\n</p><p>Subsequent follow-up recessions in 2010&#x2011;2013 were confined to Belize, El Salvador, Paraguay, Jamaica, Japan, Taiwan, New Zealand and 24 out of 50 <a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_and_dependent_territories_in_Europe" title="List of sovereign states and dependent territories in Europe">European countries</a> (including Greece). As of October 2014, only five out of the 71 countries with available quarterly data (Cyprus, Italy, Croatia, Belize and El Salvador), were still in ongoing recessions.<sup id="cite_ref-stats.oecd.org_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stats.oecd.org-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Eurostat_quarterly_GDPs_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eurostat_quarterly_GDPs-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The many follow-up recessions hitting the European countries, are commonly referred to as being direct repercussions of the <a
